<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Wildfire</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about outdoor fires. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Wildfire_(disambiguation)" title="Wildfire (disambiguation)">Wildfire (disambiguation)</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Brushfire_(disambiguation)" title="Brushfire (disambiguation)">Brushfire (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wildfire_in_California.jpg"><img alt="A daytime fire engulfing large trees" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="488" data-file-width="737" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Wildfire_in_California.jpg/300px-Wildfire_in_California.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Wildfire_in_California.jpg/450px-Wildfire_in_California.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Wildfire_in_California.jpg/600px-Wildfire_in_California.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wildfire_in_California.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wildfires_in_California" title="Wildfires in California">wildfire in California</a> on September 5, 2008</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <b>wildfire</b> or <b>wildland fire</b> is a fire in an area of combustible vegetation that occurs in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Countryside" title="Countryside">countryside</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rural_area" title="Rural area">rural area</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-1">[1]</a></sup> Depending on the type of vegetation where it occurs, a wildfire can also be classified more specifically as a <b>brush fire</b>, <b>bush fire</b>, <b>desert fire</b>, <b>forest fire</b>, <b>grass fire</b>, <b>hill fire</b>, <b>peat fire</b>, <b>vegetation fire</b>, or <b>veld fire.</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cbbcco.uk_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cbbcco.uk-2">[2]</a></sup> Fossil charcoal indicates that wildfires began soon after the appearance of terrestrial plants 420 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Wildfire’s occurrence throughout the history of terrestrial life invites conjecture that fire must have had pronounced evolutionary effects on most ecosystems' flora and fauna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4">[4]</a></sup> Earth is an intrinsically flammable planet owing to its cover of carbon-rich vegetation, seasonally dry climates, atmospheric oxygen, and widespread lightning and volcano ignitions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wildfires can be characterized in terms of the cause of ignition, their physical properties, the combustible material present, and the effect of weather on the fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UToronto_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-UToronto-5">[5]</a></sup> Wildfires can cause damage to property and human life, but they have many beneficial effects on native vegetation, animals, and ecosystems that have evolved with fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-7">[7]</a></sup> Many plant species depend on the effects of fire for growth and reproduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOVA_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOVA-8">[8]</a></sup> However, wildfire in ecosystems where wildfire is uncommon or where non-native vegetation has encroached may have negative ecological effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UToronto_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-UToronto-5">[5]</a></sup> Wildfire behaviour and severity result from the combination of factors such as available fuels, physical setting, and weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FireBehavior_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FireBehavior-11">[11]</a></sup> Analyses of historical meteorological data and national fire records in western North America show the primacy of climate in driving large regional fires via wet periods that create substantial fuels or drought and warming that extend conducive fire weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Strategies of wildfire prevention, detection, and suppression have varied over the years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> One common and inexpensive technique is <a href="/wiki/Controlled_burn" title="Controlled burn">controlled burning</a>: permitting or even igniting smaller fires to minimize the amount of flammable material available for a potential wildfire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IS_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-IS-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Vegetation may be burned periodically to maintain high species diversity and frequent burning of surface fuels limits fuel accumulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-17">[17]</a></sup> Wildland fire use is the cheapest and most ecologically appropriate policy for many forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-18">[18]</a></sup> Fuels may also be removed by logging, but fuels treatments and thinning have no effect on severe fire behavior when under extreme weather conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-19">[19]</a></sup> Wildfire itself is reportedly "the most effective treatment for reducing a fire's rate of spread, fireline intensity, flame length, and heat per unit of area" according to Jan Van Wagtendonk, a biologist at the Yellowstone Field Station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-20">[20]</a></sup> Building codes in fire-prone areas typically require that structures be built of flame-resistant materials and a <a href="/wiki/Defensible_space_(fire_control)" title="Defensible space (fire control)">defensible space</a> be maintained by clearing flammable materials within a prescribed distance from the structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-:9-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

</div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">UC Irvine scientist James Randerson discusses new research linking ocean temperatures and fire-season severity.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Four major natural causes of wildfire ignitions exist:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott2000_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scott2000-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a></li>
<li>sparks from rockfalls</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_combustion" title="Spontaneous combustion">spontaneous combustion</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanic_eruption" title="Volcanic eruption">volcanic eruption</a></li>
</ul>
<p>The most common direct human causes of wildfire ignition include <a href="/wiki/Arson" title="Arson">arson</a>, discarded cigarettes, power-line <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc" title="Electric arc">arcs</a> (as detected by <a href="/wiki/Arc_mapping" title="Arc mapping">arc mapping</a>), and sparks from equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Ignition of wildland fires via contact with hot rifle-bullet fragments is also possible under the right conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Wildfires can also be started in communities experiencing <a href="/wiki/Shifting_cultivation" title="Shifting cultivation">shifting cultivation</a>, where land is cleared quickly and farmed until the soil loses fertility, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slash_and_burn" title="Slash and burn">slash and burn</a> clearing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Forested areas cleared by logging encourage the dominance of flammable grasses, and abandoned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logging_roads" title="Logging roads">logging roads</a> overgrown by vegetation may act as fire corridors. Annual grassland fires in southern <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> stem in part from the destruction of forested areas by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_military" title="US military">US military</a> herbicides, explosives, and mechanical land-clearing and -burning operations during the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most common cause of wildfires varies throughout the world. In Canada and northwest China, for example, lightning operates as the major source of ignition. In other parts of the world, human involvement is a major contributor. In Africa, Central America, Fiji, Mexico, New Zealand, South America, and Southeast Asia, wildfires can be attributed to human activities such as agriculture, <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a>, and land-conversion burning. In China and in the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean Basin</a>, human carelessness is a major cause of wildfires. In the United States and Australia, the source of wildfires can be traced both to lightning strikes and to human activities (such as machinery sparks, cast-away cigarette butts, or <a href="/wiki/Arson" title="Arson">arson</a>)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krock_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krock-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coal_seam_fire" title="Coal seam fire">Coal seam fires</a> burn in the thousands around the world, such as those in <a href="/wiki/Burning_Mountain" title="Burning Mountain">Burning Mountain</a>, New South Wales; <a href="/wiki/Centralia,_Pennsylvania" title="Centralia, Pennsylvania">Centralia, Pennsylvania</a>; and several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_power_in_China#Coal_mine_fires" title="Coal power in China">coal-sustained fires in China</a>. They can also flare up unexpectedly and ignite nearby flammable material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread">Spread</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Spread">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wildfire_near_Cedar_Fort,_Utah.jpg"><img alt="Flat expanse of brown grasses and some green trees with black and some gray smoke and visible flames in the distance." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Wildfire_near_Cedar_Fort%2C_Utah.jpg/240px-Wildfire_near_Cedar_Fort%2C_Utah.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Wildfire_near_Cedar_Fort%2C_Utah.jpg/360px-Wildfire_near_Cedar_Fort%2C_Utah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Wildfire_near_Cedar_Fort%2C_Utah.jpg/480px-Wildfire_near_Cedar_Fort%2C_Utah.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wildfire_near_Cedar_Fort,_Utah.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A surface fire in the western desert of <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, U.S.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Forest_fire_aftermath.jpg"><img alt="Mountainous region with blackened soil and trees due to a recent fire." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1398" data-file-width="1874" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Forest_fire_aftermath.jpg/240px-Forest_fire_aftermath.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Forest_fire_aftermath.jpg/360px-Forest_fire_aftermath.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Forest_fire_aftermath.jpg/480px-Forest_fire_aftermath.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Forest_fire_aftermath.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Charred landscape following a crown fire in the <a href="/wiki/Cascade_Range" title="Cascade Range">North Cascades</a>, U.S.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The spread of wildfires varies based on the flammable material present, its vertical arrangement and moisture content, and weather conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham.2C_et_al..2C_iv_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graham.2C_et_al..2C_iv-32">[32]</a></sup> Fuel arrangement and density is governed in part by <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a>, as land shape determines factors such as available sunlight and water for plant growth. Overall, fire types can be generally characterized by their fuels as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li><b>Ground</b> fires are fed by subterranean roots, <a href="/wiki/Forest_floor" title="Forest floor">duff</a> and other buried <a href="/wiki/Organic_matter" title="Organic matter">organic matter</a>. This fuel type is especially susceptible to ignition due to spotting. Ground fires typically burn by smoldering, and can burn slowly for days to months, such as <a href="/wiki/Peat#Peat_Fires" title="Peat">peat fires</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kalimantan" title="Kalimantan">Kalimantan</a> and Eastern <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, which resulted from a <a href="/wiki/Mega_Rice_Project_(Kalimantan)" title="Mega Rice Project (Kalimantan)">riceland creation project</a> that unintentionally drained and dried the peat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Crawling</b> or <b>surface</b> fires are fueled by low-lying vegetation on the <a href="/wiki/Forest_floor" title="Forest floor">forest floor</a> such as leaf and timber litter, debris, grass, and low-lying shrubbery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> This kind of fire often burns at a relatively lower temperature than crown fires (less than 400 °C (752 °F)) and may spread at slow rate, though steep slopes and wind can accelerate the rate of spread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Ladder</b> fires consume material between low-level vegetation and tree canopies, such as small trees, downed logs, and <a href="/wiki/Vine" title="Vine">vines</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kudzu" title="Kudzu">Kudzu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_World_climbing_fern" title="Old World climbing fern">Old World climbing fern</a>, and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasive_plant" title="Invasive plant">invasive plants</a> that scale trees may also encourage ladder fires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FireInitiative_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FireInitiative-37">[37]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Crown</b>, <b>canopy</b>, or <b>aerial</b> fires burn suspended material at the canopy level, such as tall trees, vines, and mosses. The ignition of a crown fire, termed <i>crowning</i>, is dependent on the density of the suspended material, canopy height, canopy continuity, sufficient surface and ladder fires, vegetation moisture content, and weather conditions during the blaze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Stand-replacing fires lit by humans can spread into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_rain_forest" title="Amazon rain forest">Amazon rain forest</a>, damaging ecosystems not particularly suited for heat or arid conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><span id="Physical_properties"></span></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Northwest_Crown_Fire_Experiment.png"><img alt="A line of trees completely engulfed in flames. Towers with instrumentation are seen just beyond the fire's reach." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1402" data-file-width="2095" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Northwest_Crown_Fire_Experiment.png/220px-Northwest_Crown_Fire_Experiment.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Northwest_Crown_Fire_Experiment.png/330px-Northwest_Crown_Fire_Experiment.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Northwest_Crown_Fire_Experiment.png/440px-Northwest_Crown_Fire_Experiment.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Northwest_Crown_Fire_Experiment.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Experimental fire in Canada</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">Combustion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fire_control" title="Fire control">Fire control</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">Extreme weather</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Firestorm" title="Firestorm">Firestorm</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comtrasts.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2242" data-file-width="3992" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Comtrasts.jpg/220px-Comtrasts.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Comtrasts.jpg/330px-Comtrasts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Comtrasts.jpg/440px-Comtrasts.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comtrasts.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A dirt road acted as a fire barrier in South Africa. The effects of the barrier can clearly be seen on the unburnt (left) and burnt (right) sides of the road.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wildfires occur when all of the necessary elements of a <a href="/wiki/Fire_triangle" title="Fire triangle">fire triangle</a> come together in a susceptible area: an ignition source is brought into contact with a combustible material such as <a href="/wiki/Vegetation" title="Vegetation">vegetation</a>, that is subjected to sufficient heat and has an adequate supply of oxygen from the ambient air. A high moisture content usually prevents ignition and slows propagation, because higher temperatures are required to evaporate any water within the material and heat the material to its <a href="/wiki/Fire_point" title="Fire point">fire point</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FireBehavior_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FireBehavior-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIFC_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIFC-40">[40]</a></sup> Dense forests usually provide more shade, resulting in lower ambient temperatures and greater humidity, and are therefore less susceptible to wildfires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham.2C_et_al..2C_12_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graham.2C_et_al..2C_12-41">[41]</a></sup> Less dense material such as grasses and leaves are easier to ignite because they contain less water than denser material such as branches and trunks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-42">[42]</a></sup> Plants continuously lose water by <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a>, but water loss is usually balanced by water absorbed from the soil, humidity, or rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> When this balance is not maintained, plants dry out and are therefore more flammable, often a consequence of droughts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Structure_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Structure-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>A wildfire <i>front</i> is the portion sustaining continuous flaming combustion, where unburned material meets active flames, or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smolder" title="Smolder">smoldering</a> transition between unburned and burned material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> As the front approaches, the fire heats both the surrounding air and woody material through <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">thermal radiation</a>. First, wood is dried as water is vaporized at a temperature of 100 °C (212 °F). Next, the <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a> of wood at 230 °C (450 °F) releases flammable gases. Finally, wood can smoulder at 380 °C (720 °F) or, when heated sufficiently, ignite at 590 °C (1,000 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Even before the flames of a wildfire arrive at a particular location, <a href="/wiki/Heat_transfer" title="Heat transfer">heat transfer</a> from the wildfire front warms the air to 800 °C (1,470 °F), which pre-heats and dries flammable materials, causing materials to ignite faster and allowing the fire to spread faster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FireScars_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FireScars-49">[49]</a></sup> High-temperature and long-duration surface wildfires may encourage <a href="/wiki/Flashover" title="Flashover">flashover</a> or <i>torching</i>: the drying of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canopy_(ecology)" title="Canopy (ecology)">tree canopies</a> and their subsequent ignition from below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wildfires have a rapid <i>forward rate of spread</i> (FROS) when burning through dense, uninterrupted fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> They can move as fast as 10.8 kilometres per hour (6.7 mph) in forests and 22 kilometres per hour (14 mph) in grasslands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Wildfires can advance tangential to the main front to form a <i>flanking</i> front, or burn in the opposite direction of the main front by <i>backing</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> They may also spread by <i>jumping</i> or <i>spotting</i> as winds and vertical <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a> columns carry <i>firebrands</i> (hot wood embers) and other burning materials through the air over roads, rivers, and other barriers that may otherwise act as <a href="/wiki/Firebreak" title="Firebreak">firebreaks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-underfire_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-underfire-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Torching and fires in tree canopies encourage spotting, and dry ground fuels that surround a wildfire are especially vulnerable to ignition from firebrands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Spotting can create <i>spot fires</i> as hot embers and firebrands ignite fuels downwind from the fire. In Australian bushfires, spot fires are known to occur as far as 20 kilometres (12 mi) from the fire front.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kilmore-East_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kilmore-East-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Especially large wildfires may affect air currents in their immediate vicinities by the <a href="/wiki/Stack_effect" title="Stack effect">stack effect</a>: air rises as it is heated, and large wildfires create powerful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Updraft" title="Updraft">updrafts</a> that will <a href="/wiki/Advection" title="Advection">draw in</a> new, cooler air from surrounding areas in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_column" title="Thermal column">thermal columns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Great vertical differences in temperature and humidity encourage <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrocumulus_cloud" title="Pyrocumulus cloud">pyrocumulus clouds</a>, strong winds, and <a href="/wiki/Fire_whirl" title="Fire whirl">fire whirls</a> with the force of tornadoes at speeds of more than 80 kilometres per hour (50 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Rapid rates of spread, prolific crowning or spotting, the presence of fire whirls, and strong convection columns signify extreme conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The thermal heat from wildfire can cause significant <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> of rocks and boulders, heat can rapidly expand a boulder and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_shock" title="Thermal shock">thermal shock</a> can occur, which may cause an object's structure to fail.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_of_weather">Effect of weather</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Effect of weather">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2011-08-04_20_00_00_Susie_Fire_in_the_Adobe_Range_west_of_Elko_Nevada.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/2011-08-04_20_00_00_Susie_Fire_in_the_Adobe_Range_west_of_Elko_Nevada.jpg/220px-2011-08-04_20_00_00_Susie_Fire_in_the_Adobe_Range_west_of_Elko_Nevada.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/2011-08-04_20_00_00_Susie_Fire_in_the_Adobe_Range_west_of_Elko_Nevada.jpg/330px-2011-08-04_20_00_00_Susie_Fire_in_the_Adobe_Range_west_of_Elko_Nevada.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/2011-08-04_20_00_00_Susie_Fire_in_the_Adobe_Range_west_of_Elko_Nevada.jpg/440px-2011-08-04_20_00_00_Susie_Fire_in_the_Adobe_Range_west_of_Elko_Nevada.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2011-08-04_20_00_00_Susie_Fire_in_the_Adobe_Range_west_of_Elko_Nevada.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lightning-sparked wildfires are frequent occurrences during the dry summer season in <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Incendio_en_Caracas_(4515878847).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Incendio_en_Caracas_%284515878847%29.jpg/220px-Incendio_en_Caracas_%284515878847%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Incendio_en_Caracas_%284515878847%29.jpg/330px-Incendio_en_Caracas_%284515878847%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Incendio_en_Caracas_%284515878847%29.jpg/440px-Incendio_en_Caracas_%284515878847%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Incendio_en_Caracas_(4515878847).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A wildfire in Venezuela during a drought</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Heat_wave" title="Heat wave">Heat waves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">droughts</a>, cyclical <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate changes</a> such as <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a>, and regional weather patterns such as high-pressure ridges can increase the risk and alter the behavior of wildfires dramatically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Years of precipitation followed by warm periods can encourage more widespread fires and longer fire seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Since the mid-1980s, earlier snowmelt and associated warming has also been associated with an increase in length and severity of the wildfire season in the <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">Western United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Global warming</a> may increase the intensity and frequency of droughts in many areas, creating more intense and frequent wildfires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UToronto_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-UToronto-5">[5]</a></sup> A 2015 study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> indicates that the increase in fire risk in California may be attributable to human-induced climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> A study of alluvial sediment deposits going back over 8,000 years found warmer climate periods experienced severe droughts and stand-replacing fires and concluded climate was such a powerful influence on wildfire that trying to recreate presettlement forest structure is likely impossible in a warmer future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Intensity also increases during daytime hours. Burn rates of smoldering logs are up to five times greater during the day due to lower humidity, increased temperatures, and increased wind speeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Sunlight warms the ground during the day which creates air currents that travel uphill. At night the land cools, creating air currents that travel downhill. Wildfires are fanned by these winds and often follow the air currents over hills and through valleys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Fires in Europe occur frequently during the hours of 12:00 p.m. and 2:00 p.m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Wildfire suppression operations in the United States revolve around a 24-hour <i>fire day</i> that begins at 10:00 a.m. due to the predictable increase in intensity resulting from the daytime warmth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fire_ecology" title="Fire ecology">Fire ecology</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Disturbance_(ecology)" title="Disturbance (ecology)">Disturbance (ecology)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forestry" title="Forestry">Forestry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Fires_-_August_and_February_2008.jpg"><img alt="Two illustrations of the earth, one above the other. The seas are dark gray in color and the continents a lighter gray. Both images have red, yellow, and white markers indicating where fires occurred during the months of August (top image) and February (bottom image) of the year 2008." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="870" data-file-width="737" height="519" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Global_Fires_-_August_and_February_2008.jpg/440px-Global_Fires_-_August_and_February_2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Global_Fires_-_August_and_February_2008.jpg/660px-Global_Fires_-_August_and_February_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/19/Global_Fires_-_August_and_February_2008.jpg 2x" width="440"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_Fires_-_August_and_February_2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Global fires during the year 2008 for the months of August (top image) and February (bottom image), as detected by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moderate_Resolution_Imaging_Spectroradiometer" title="Moderate Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer">Moderate Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer</a> (MODIS) on NASA's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terra_satellite" title="Terra satellite">Terra satellite</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wildfire’s occurrence throughout the history of terrestrial life invites conjecture that fire must have had pronounced evolutionary effects on most ecosystems' flora and fauna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4">[4]</a></sup> Wildfires are common in climates that are sufficiently moist to allow the growth of vegetation but feature extended dry, hot periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOVA_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-NOVA-8">[8]</a></sup> Such places include the vegetated areas of Australia and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Veld" title="Veld">veld</a> in southern Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Fynbos" title="Fynbos">fynbos</a> in the Western Cape of South Africa, the forested areas of the United States and Canada, and the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean Basin</a>.</p>
<p>High-severity wildfire creates <a href="/wiki/Complex_early_seral_forest" title="Complex early seral forest">complex early seral forest</a> habitat (also called “snag forest habitat”), which often has higher species richness and diversity than unburned old forest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-6">[6]</a></sup> Plant and animal species in most types of North American forests evolved with fire, and many of these species depend on wildfires, and particularly high-severity fires, to reproduce and grow. Fire helps to return nutrients from plant matter back to soil, the heat from fire is necessary to the germination of certain types of seeds, and the snags (dead trees) and early successional forests created by high-severity fire create habitat conditions that are beneficial to wildlife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-6">[6]</a></sup> Early successional forests created by high-severity fire support some of the highest levels of native biodiversity found in temperate conifer forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Post-fire logging has no ecological benefits and many negative impacts; the same is often true for post-fire seeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although some ecosystems rely on naturally occurring fires to regulate growth, some ecosystems suffer from too much fire, such as the <a href="/wiki/Chaparral" title="Chaparral">chaparral</a> in <a href="/wiki/Southern_California" title="Southern California">southern California</a> and lower elevation deserts in the American Southwest. The increased fire frequency in these ordinarily fire-dependent areas has upset natural cycles, damaged native plant communities, and encouraged the growth of non-native weeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">Invasive species</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Lygodium_microphyllum" title="Lygodium microphyllum">Lygodium microphyllum</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Bromus_tectorum" title="Bromus tectorum">Bromus tectorum</a></i>, can grow rapidly in areas that were damaged by fires. Because they are highly flammable, they can increase the future risk of fire, creating a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Positive_feedback_loop" title="Positive feedback loop">positive feedback loop</a> that increases fire frequency and further alters native vegetation communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FireInitiative_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FireInitiative-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_Wagtendonk.2C_14_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_Wagtendonk.2C_14-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_Rainforest" title="Amazon Rainforest">Amazon Rainforest</a>, drought, logging, cattle ranching practices, and <a href="/wiki/Slash-and-burn" title="Slash-and-burn">slash-and-burn</a> agriculture damage fire-resistant forests and promote the growth of flammable brush, creating a cycle that encourages more burning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Fires in the rainforest threaten its collection of diverse species and produce large amounts of CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Also, fires in the rainforest, along with drought and human involvement, could damage or destroy more than half of the Amazon rainforest by the year 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Wildfires generate ash, destroy available organic nutrients, and cause an increase in water runoff, eroding away other nutrients and creating <a href="/wiki/Flash_flood" title="Flash flood">flash flood</a> conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham.2C_et_al..2C_iv_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Graham.2C_et_al..2C_iv-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> A 2003 wildfire in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Yorkshire_Moors" title="North Yorkshire Moors">North Yorkshire Moors</a> destroyed 2.5 square kilometers (600 acres) of <a href="/wiki/Ericaceae" title="Ericaceae">heather</a> and the underlying <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a> layers. Afterwards, wind erosion stripped the ash and the exposed soil, revealing archaeological remains dating back to 10,000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Wildfires can also have an effect on climate change, increasing the amount of carbon released into the atmosphere and inhibiting vegetation growth, which affects overall carbon uptake by plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a> there is a natural pattern of accumulation of fuel and wildfire which varies depending on the nature of vegetation and terrain. Research in Alaska has shown fire-event return intervals, (FRIs) that typically vary from 150 to 200 years with dryer lowland areas burning more frequently than wetter upland areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plant_adaptation">Plant adaptation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Plant adaptation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boreal_pine_forest_after_fire.JPG"><img alt="Two photographs of the same section of a pine forest; both show blackened bark at least halfway up the trees. The first picture is noticeably lacking in surface vegetation, while the second shows small, green grasses on the forest floor." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="5800" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Boreal_pine_forest_after_fire.JPG/440px-Boreal_pine_forest_after_fire.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Boreal_pine_forest_after_fire.JPG/660px-Boreal_pine_forest_after_fire.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Boreal_pine_forest_after_fire.JPG/880px-Boreal_pine_forest_after_fire.JPG 2x" width="440"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boreal_pine_forest_after_fire.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ecological_succession" title="Ecological succession">Ecological succession</a> after a wildfire in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boreal_forest" title="Boreal forest">boreal pine forest</a> next to Hara Bog, <a href="/wiki/Lahemaa_National_Park" title="Lahemaa National Park">Lahemaa National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>. The pictures were taken one and two years after the fire.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Plants in wildfire-prone <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystems" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a> often survive through adaptations to their local <a href="/wiki/Fire_regime" title="Fire regime">fire regime</a>. Such adaptations include physical protection against heat, increased growth after a fire event, and flammable materials that encourage fire and may eliminate <a href="/wiki/Competition" title="Competition">competition</a>. For example, plants of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus" title="Eucalyptus">Eucalyptus</a></i> contain flammable oils that encourage fire and hard <a href="/wiki/Sclerophyll" title="Sclerophyll">sclerophyll</a> leaves to resist heat and drought, ensuring their dominance over less fire-tolerant species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Dense bark, shedding lower branches, and high water content in external structures may also protect trees from rising temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOVA_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-NOVA-8">[8]</a></sup> Fire-resistant seeds and reserve <a href="/wiki/Shoot" title="Shoot">shoots</a> that sprout after a fire encourage species preservation, as embodied by <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_species" title="Pioneer species">pioneer species</a>. Smoke, charred wood, and heat can stimulate the germination of seeds in a process called <i><a href="/wiki/Serotiny" title="Serotiny">serotiny</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Exposure to smoke from burning plants promotes <a href="/wiki/Germination" title="Germination">germination</a> in other types of plants by inducing the production of the orange <a href="/wiki/Butenolide" title="Butenolide">butenolide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flematti_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-flematti-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Grasslands in Western <a href="/wiki/Sabah" title="Sabah">Sabah</a>, Malaysian <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine</a> forests, and Indonesian <i><a href="/wiki/Casuarina" title="Casuarina">Casuarina</a></i> forests are believed to have resulted from previous periods of fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chamise" title="Chamise">Chamise</a> deadwood litter is low in water content and flammable, and the shrub quickly sprouts after a fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOVA_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-NOVA-8">[8]</a></sup> Cape lilies lie dormant until flames brush away the covering, then blossom almost overnight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sequoia_sempervirens" title="Sequoia sempervirens">Sequoia</a> rely on periodic fires to reduce competition, release seeds from their <a href="/wiki/Conifer_cone" title="Conifer cone">cones</a>, and clear the soil and canopy for new growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caribbean_Pine" title="Caribbean Pine">Caribbean Pine</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bahamian_pineyards" title="Bahamian pineyards">Bahamian pineyards</a> have adapted to and rely on low-intensity, surface fires for survival and growth. An optimum fire frequency for growth is every 3 to 10 years. Too frequent fires favor <a href="/wiki/Herbaceous_plant" title="Herbaceous plant">herbaceous plants</a>, and infrequent fires favor species typical of <a href="/wiki/Bahamian_dry_forests" title="Bahamian dry forests">Bahamian dry forests</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_effects">Atmospheric effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Atmospheric effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">Air pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_chemistry" title="Atmospheric chemistry">Atmospheric chemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haze" title="Haze">Haze</a>, <a href="/wiki/1997_Southeast_Asian_haze" title="1997 Southeast Asian haze">1997 Southeast Asian haze</a>, <a href="/wiki/2005_Malaysian_haze" title="2005 Malaysian haze">2005 Malaysian haze</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">Carbon cycle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wildfire_in_Yellowstone_NP_produces_Pyrocumulus_cloud.jpg"><img alt="A paved road with trees and grasses on the side with a large, white and dark gray smoke cloud rising in the distance." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1444" data-file-width="2100" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Wildfire_in_Yellowstone_NP_produces_Pyrocumulus_cloud.jpg/240px-Wildfire_in_Yellowstone_NP_produces_Pyrocumulus_cloud.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Wildfire_in_Yellowstone_NP_produces_Pyrocumulus_cloud.jpg/360px-Wildfire_in_Yellowstone_NP_produces_Pyrocumulus_cloud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Wildfire_in_Yellowstone_NP_produces_Pyrocumulus_cloud.jpg/480px-Wildfire_in_Yellowstone_NP_produces_Pyrocumulus_cloud.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wildfire_in_Yellowstone_NP_produces_Pyrocumulus_cloud.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrocumulus_cloud" title="Pyrocumulus cloud">Pyrocumulus cloud</a> produced by a wildfire in <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most of the Earth's weather and air pollution resides in the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a>, the part of the atmosphere that extends from the surface of the planet to a height of about 10 kilometers (6 mi). The vertical lift of a severe thunderstorm or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrocumulonimbus" title="Pyrocumulonimbus">pyrocumulonimbus</a> can be enhanced in the area of a large wildfire, which can propel smoke, soot, and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_particulate_matter" title="Atmospheric particulate matter">particulate matter</a> as high as the lower <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Previously, prevailing scientific theory held that most particles in the stratosphere came from <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a>, but smoke and other wildfire emissions have been detected from the lower stratosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Pyrocumulus clouds can reach 6,100 meters (20,000 ft) over wildfires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Satellite observation of smoke plumes from wildfires revealed that the plumes could be traced intact for distances exceeding 1,600 kilometers (1,000 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Computer-aided models such as <a href="/wiki/CALPUFF" title="CALPUFF">CALPUFF</a> may help predict the size and direction of wildfire-generated smoke plumes by using <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_dispersion_modeling" title="Atmospheric dispersion modeling">atmospheric dispersion modeling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wildfires can affect local atmospheric pollution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> and release carbon in the form of carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wildland_fire_emissions" title="Wildland fire emissions">Wildfire emissions</a> contain fine particulate matter which can cause cardiovascular and respiratory problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Increased fire byproducts in the troposphere can increase ozone concentration beyond safe levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Forest fires in Indonesia in 1997 were estimated to have released between 0.81 and 2.57 giga<a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> (0.89 and 2.83 billion <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a>) of CO<sub>2</sub> into the atmosphere, which is between 13%–40% of the annual global carbon dioxide emissions from burning fossil fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Atmospheric models suggest that these concentrations of sooty particles could increase absorption of incoming <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a> during winter months by as much as 15%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
