<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fossil_record_of_fire" title="Fossil record of fire">Fossil record of fire</a></div>
<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Welsh_Borders" title="Welsh Borders">Welsh Borders</a>, the first evidence of wildfire is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhyniophyta" title="Rhyniophyta">rhyniophytoid</a> plant fossils preserved as <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a>, dating to the <a href="/wiki/Silurian" title="Silurian">Silurian</a> period (about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=420">420</a> million years ago</span>). Smoldering surface fires started to occur sometime before the Early <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> period <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=405">405</a> million years ago</span>. Low atmospheric oxygen during the Middle and Late Devonian was accompanied by a decrease in charcoal abundance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glasspool_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glasspool-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Additional charcoal evidence suggests that fires continued through the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> period. Later, the overall increase of atmospheric oxygen from 13% in the Late Devonian to 30-31% by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Permian" title="Late Permian">Late Permian</a> was accompanied by a more widespread distribution of wildfires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott2006_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scott2006-109">[109]</a></sup> Later, a decrease in wildfire-related charcoal deposits from the late Permian to the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> periods is explained by a decrease in oxygen levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pausas_594_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pausas_594-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wildfires during the Paleozoic and Mesozoic periods followed patterns similar to fires that occur in modern times. Surface fires driven by dry seasons<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> are evident in Devonian and Carboniferous <a href="/wiki/Progymnosperm" title="Progymnosperm">progymnosperm</a> forests. <a href="/wiki/Lepidodendron" title="Lepidodendron">Lepidodendron</a> forests dating to the Carboniferous period have charred peaks, evidence of crown fires. In Jurassic <a href="/wiki/Gymnosperm" title="Gymnosperm">gymnosperm</a> forests, there is evidence of high frequency, light surface fires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pausas_594_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pausas_594-110">[110]</a></sup> The increase of fire activity in the late <a href="/wiki/Tertiary" title="Tertiary">Tertiary</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cenozoic-division_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-cenozoic-division-111">[111]</a></sup> is possibly due to the increase of <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C<sub>4</sub></a>-type grasses. As these grasses shifted to more <a href="/wiki/Mesic_habitat" title="Mesic habitat">mesic habitats</a>, their high flammability increased fire frequency, promoting grasslands over woodlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> However, fire-prone habitats may have contributed to the prominence of trees such as those of the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus" title="Eucalyptus">Eucalyptus</a></i>, <i>Pinus</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sequoia_(genus)" title="Sequoia (genus)">Sequoia</a></i>, which have thick bark to withstand fires and employ <a href="/wiki/Serotiny" title="Serotiny">serotiny</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_involvement">Human involvement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Human involvement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Control_of_fire_by_early_humans" title="Control of fire by early humans">Control of fire by early humans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation#Historical_causes" title="Deforestation">Deforestation § Historical causes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_history" title="Environmental history">Environmental history</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_firefighting" title="History of firefighting">History of firefighting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_wildfires" title="List of wildfires">List of wildfires</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Burning_mountains_Thailand.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1272" data-file-width="1920" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Burning_mountains_Thailand.JPG/260px-Burning_mountains_Thailand.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Burning_mountains_Thailand.JPG/390px-Burning_mountains_Thailand.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Burning_mountains_Thailand.JPG/520px-Burning_mountains_Thailand.JPG 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Burning_mountains_Thailand.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Aerial view of deliberate wildfires on the <a href="/wiki/Khun_Tan_Range" title="Khun Tan Range">Khun Tan Range</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>. These fires are lit by local farmers every year in order to promote the growth of a certain mushroom</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The human use of fire for agricultural and hunting purposes during the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> ages altered the preexisting landscapes and fire regimes. Woodlands were gradually replaced by smaller vegetation that facilitated travel, hunting, seed-gathering and planting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> In recorded human history, minor allusions to wildfires were mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> and by classical writers such as <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>. However, while ancient Hebrew, Greek, and Roman writers were aware of fires, they were not very interested in the uncultivated lands where wildfires occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RackhamFire_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-RackhamFire-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RackhamHist_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-RackhamHist-117">[117]</a></sup> Wildfires were used in battles throughout human history as <a href="/wiki/Early_thermal_weapons" title="Early thermal weapons">early thermal weapons</a>. From the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_ages" title="Middle ages">Middle ages</a>, accounts were written of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occupational_burning" title="Occupational burning">occupational burning</a> as well as customs and laws that governed the use of fire. In Germany, regular burning was documented in 1290 in the <a href="/wiki/Odenwald" title="Odenwald">Odenwald</a> and in 1344 in the <a href="/wiki/Black_Forest" title="Black Forest">Black Forest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldammer_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldammer-118">[118]</a></sup> In the 14th century <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a>, firebreaks were used for wildfire protection. In Spain during the 1550s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheep_husbandry" title="Sheep husbandry">sheep husbandry</a> was discouraged in certain provinces by <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip II</a> due to the harmful effects of fires used in <a href="/wiki/Transhumance" title="Transhumance">transhumance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RackhamFire_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-RackhamFire-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RackhamHist_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-RackhamHist-117">[117]</a></sup> As early as the 17th century, Native Americans were observed <a href="/wiki/Native_American_use_of_fire" title="Native American use of fire">using fire</a> for many purposes including cultivation, <a href="/wiki/Smoke_signal" title="Smoke signal">signaling</a>, and warfare. Scottish botanist <a href="/wiki/David_Douglas_(botanist)" title="David Douglas (botanist)">David Douglas</a> noted the native use of fire for tobacco cultivation, to encourage deer into smaller areas for hunting purposes, and to improve foraging for honey and grasshoppers. Charcoal found in sedimentary deposits off the Pacific coast of Central America suggests that more burning occurred in the 50 years before the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">Spanish colonization of the Americas</a> than after the colonization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> In the post-World War II <a href="/wiki/Baltic_region" title="Baltic region">Baltic region</a>, socio-economic changes led more stringent air quality standards and bans on fires that eliminated traditional burning practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldammer_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goldammer-118">[118]</a></sup> In the mid-19th century, explorers from the <a href="/wiki/HMS_Beagle" title="HMS Beagle">HMS <i>Beagle</i></a> observed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_Aborigines" title="Australian Aborigines">Australian Aborigines</a> using fire for ground clearing, hunting, and regeneration of plant food in a method later named <a href="/wiki/Fire-stick_farming" title="Fire-stick farming">fire-stick farming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Such careful use of fire has been employed for centuries in the lands protected by <a href="/wiki/Kakadu_National_Park" title="Kakadu National Park">Kakadu National Park</a> to encourage biodiversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wildfires typically occurred during periods of increased temperature and drought. An increase in fire-related <a href="/wiki/Debris_flow" title="Debris flow">debris flow</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alluvial_fan" title="Alluvial fan">alluvial fans</a> of northeastern <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a> was linked to the period between AD 1050 and 1200, coinciding with the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">Medieval Warm Period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> However, human influence caused an increase in fire frequency. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dendrochronological" title="Dendrochronological">Dendrochronological</a> fire scar data and charcoal layer data in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> suggests that, while many fires occurred during severe drought conditions, an increase in the number of fires during 850 BC and 1660 AD can be attributed to human influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Charcoal evidence from the Americas suggested a general decrease in wildfires between 1 AD and 1750 compared to previous years. However, a period of increased fire frequency between 1750 and 1870 was suggested by charcoal data from North America and Asia, attributed to human population growth and influences such as land clearing practices. This period was followed by an overall decrease in burning in the 20th century, linked to the expansion of agriculture, increased livestock grazing, and fire prevention efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> A meta-analysis found that 17 times more land burned annually in California before 1800 compared to recent decades (1,800,000 hectares/year compared to 102,000 hectares/year).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a paper published in <a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a>, the number of natural and human-caused fires decreased by 24.3% between 1998 and 2015. Researchers explain this a transition from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nomadism" title="Nomadism">nomadism</a> to settled lifestyle and intensification of <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> that lead to a drop in the use of fire for land clearing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">Invasive species</a> moved by humans have in some cases increased the intensity of wildfires, such as <a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus" title="Eucalyptus">Eucalyptus</a> in California and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamba_grass" title="Gamba grass">gamba grass</a> in Australia.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fire_protection" title="Fire protection">Fire protection</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Smokey3.jpg"><img alt='Drawing of a grizzly bear with human features. He is wearing blue jeans with a belt and a brimmed hat with the name "Smokey" on the cap, and has a shovel in his left hand. He is pointing to the viewer while the text "Only You" is seen below him.' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="450" height="352" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Smokey3.jpg/240px-Smokey3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Smokey3.jpg/360px-Smokey3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ae/Smokey3.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Smokey3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1985 <a href="/wiki/Smokey_Bear" title="Smokey Bear">Smokey Bear</a> poster with part of his admonition, "Only you can prevent forest fires".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wildfire prevention refers to the preemptive methods aimed at reducing the risk of fires as well as lessening its severity and spread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Prevention techniques aim to manage air quality, maintain ecological balances, protect resources,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_Wagtendonk.2C_14_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-van_Wagtendonk.2C_14-79">[79]</a></sup> and to affect future fires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> North American firefighting policies permit naturally caused fires to burn to maintain their ecological role, so long as the risks of escape into high-value areas are mitigated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> However, prevention policies must consider the role that humans play in wildfires, since, for example, 95% of forest fires in Europe are related to human involvement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Sources of human-caused fire may include arson, accidental ignition, or the uncontrolled use of fire in land-clearing and agriculture such as the slash-and-burn farming in Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1937, U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> initiated a nationwide fire prevention campaign, highlighting the role of human carelessness in forest fires. Later posters of the program featured <a href="/wiki/Uncle_Sam" title="Uncle Sam">Uncle Sam</a>, leaders of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axis_powers_of_World_War_II" title="Axis powers of World War II">Axis powers of World War II</a>, characters from the Disney movie <i><a href="/wiki/Bambi" title="Bambi">Bambi</a></i>, and the official mascot of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Forest_Service" title="U.S. Forest Service">U.S. Forest Service</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smokey_Bear" title="Smokey Bear">Smokey Bear</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Reducing human-caused ignitions may be the most effective means of reducing unwanted wildfire. Alteration of fuels is commonly undertaken when attempting to affect future fire risk and behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham.2C_et_al..2C_iv_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Graham.2C_et_al..2C_iv-32">[32]</a></sup> Wildfire prevention programs around the world may employ techniques such as <i>wildland fire use</i> and <i>prescribed or <a href="/wiki/Controlled_burn" title="Controlled burn">controlled burns</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> <i>Wildland fire use</i> refers to any fire of natural causes that is monitored but allowed to burn. <i>Controlled burns</i> are fires ignited by government agencies under less dangerous weather conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prescribed_burn_in_a_Pinus_nigra_stand_in_Portugal.JPG"><img alt="A small fire on the slope of a hill. The hill features small, green shubbery and some trees. A person in light-colored clothing in seen in the background, some distance from the flames." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Prescribed_burn_in_a_Pinus_nigra_stand_in_Portugal.JPG/240px-Prescribed_burn_in_a_Pinus_nigra_stand_in_Portugal.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Prescribed_burn_in_a_Pinus_nigra_stand_in_Portugal.JPG/360px-Prescribed_burn_in_a_Pinus_nigra_stand_in_Portugal.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Prescribed_burn_in_a_Pinus_nigra_stand_in_Portugal.JPG/480px-Prescribed_burn_in_a_Pinus_nigra_stand_in_Portugal.JPG 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prescribed_burn_in_a_Pinus_nigra_stand_in_Portugal.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A prescribed burn in a <i><a href="/wiki/Pinus_nigra" title="Pinus nigra">Pinus nigra</a></i> stand in <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Vegetation may be burned periodically to maintain high species diversity and frequent burning of surface fuels limits fuel accumulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-17">[17]</a></sup> Wildland fire use is the cheapest and most ecologically appropriate policy for many forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-:5-18">[18]</a></sup> Fuels may also be removed by logging, but fuels treatments and thinning have no effect on severe fire behavior<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-19">[19]</a></sup> Wildfire models are often used to predict and compare the benefits of different fuel treatments on future wildfire spread, but their accuracy is low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham.2C_et_al..2C_iv_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Graham.2C_et_al..2C_iv-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wildfire itself is reportedly "the most effective treatment for reducing a fire's rate of spread, fireline intensity, flame length, and heat per unit of area" according to Jan van Wagtendonk, a biologist at the Yellowstone Field Station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Building codes in fire-prone areas typically require that structures be built of flame-resistant materials and a <a href="/wiki/Defensible_space_(fire_control)" title="Defensible space (fire control)">defensible space</a> be maintained by clearing flammable materials within a prescribed distance from the structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-:8-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-:9-22">[22]</a></sup> Communities in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> also maintain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire_line" title="Fire line">fire lines</a> 5 to 10 meters (16 to 33 ft) wide between the forest and their village, and patrol these lines during summer months or seasons of dry weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Continued residential development in fire-prone areas and rebuilding structures destroyed by fires has been met with criticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trial2_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trial2-138">[138]</a></sup> The ecological benefits of fire are often overridden by the economic and safety benefits of protecting structures and human life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Detection">Detection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Detection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">Remote sensing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Drymountainlookout1930.jpg"><img alt="A four-legged tower with a small at the top, next to two one-story buildings. The tower is four stories tall. Trees are at either side, and in the foreground there are rocks, some vegetation, and a rough trail." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="379" height="336" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Drymountainlookout1930.jpg/240px-Drymountainlookout1930.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Drymountainlookout1930.jpg/360px-Drymountainlookout1930.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/Drymountainlookout1930.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Drymountainlookout1930.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dry Mountain Fire Lookout in the <a href="/wiki/Ochoco_National_Forest" title="Ochoco National Forest">Ochoco National Forest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, circa 1930</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Fast and effective detection is a key factor in wildfire fighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Early detection efforts were focused on early response, accurate results in both daytime and nighttime, and the ability to prioritize fire danger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Integration_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Integration-141">[141]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fire_lookout_tower" title="Fire lookout tower">Fire lookout towers</a> were used in the United States in the early 20th century and fires were reported using telephones, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carrier_pigeon" title="Carrier pigeon">carrier pigeons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Heliograph" title="Heliograph">heliographs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Aerial and land photography using <a href="/wiki/Instant_camera" title="Instant camera">instant cameras</a> were used in the 1950s until <a href="/wiki/Infrared_photography" title="Infrared photography">infrared scanning</a> was developed for fire detection in the 1960s. However, information analysis and delivery was often delayed by limitations in communication technology. Early satellite-derived fire analyses were hand-drawn on maps at a remote site and sent via overnight mail to the <a href="/wiki/Incident_commander" title="Incident commander">fire manager</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_fires_of_1988" title="Yellowstone fires of 1988">Yellowstone fires of 1988</a>, a data station was established in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Yellowstone" title="West Yellowstone">West Yellowstone</a>, permitting the delivery of satellite-based fire information in approximately four hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Integration_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Integration-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Currently, public hotlines, <a href="/wiki/Fire_lookout" title="Fire lookout">fire lookouts</a> in towers, and ground and aerial patrols can be used as a means of early detection of forest fires. However, accurate human observation may be limited by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asthenopia" title="Asthenopia">operator fatigue</a>, time of day, time of year, and geographic location. Electronic systems have gained popularity in recent years as a possible resolution to human operator error. A government report on a recent trial of three automated camera fire detection systems in Australia did, however, conclude "...detection by the camera systems was slower and less reliable than by a trained human observer". These systems may be semi- or fully automated and employ systems based on the risk area and degree of human presence, as suggested by <a href="/wiki/Geographic_information_system" title="Geographic information system">GIS</a> data analyses. An integrated approach of multiple systems can be used to merge satellite data, aerial imagery, and personnel position via <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">Global Positioning System</a> (GPS) into a collective whole for near-realtime use by wireless <a href="/wiki/Incident_Command_System" title="Incident Command System">Incident Command Centers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>A small, high risk area that features thick vegetation, a strong human presence, or is close to a critical urban area can be monitored using a local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensor_network" title="Sensor network">sensor network</a>. Detection systems may include <a href="/wiki/Wireless_sensor_network" title="Wireless sensor network">wireless sensor networks</a> that act as automated weather systems: detecting temperature, humidity, and smoke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> These may be battery-powered, solar-powered, or <i>tree-rechargeable</i>: able to recharge their battery systems using the small electrical currents in plant material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Larger, medium-risk areas can be monitored by scanning towers that incorporate fixed cameras and sensors to detect smoke or additional factors such as the infrared signature of carbon dioxide produced by fires. Additional capabilities such as <a href="/wiki/Night_vision" title="Night vision">night vision</a>, brightness detection, and color change detection may also be incorporated into sensor arrays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wildfires_Balkans_July_2007-NASA.jpg"><img alt="A satellite view of the Balkans and Greece. Clouds and smoke trails are seen above the Balkans and trailing south into the Ionian Sea." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5200" data-file-width="4600" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Wildfires_Balkans_July_2007-NASA.jpg/240px-Wildfires_Balkans_July_2007-NASA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Wildfires_Balkans_July_2007-NASA.jpg/360px-Wildfires_Balkans_July_2007-NASA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Wildfires_Balkans_July_2007-NASA.jpg/480px-Wildfires_Balkans_July_2007-NASA.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wildfires_Balkans_July_2007-NASA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wildfires across the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> in late July 2007 (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MODIS" title="MODIS">MODIS</a> image)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Satellite and aerial monitoring through the use of planes, helicopter, or UAVs can provide a wider view and may be sufficient to monitor very large, low risk areas. These more sophisticated systems employ GPS and aircraft-mounted infrared or high-resolution visible cameras to identify and target wildfires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Satellite-mounted sensors such as <a href="/wiki/Envisat" title="Envisat">Envisat</a>'s <a href="/wiki/AATSR" title="AATSR">Advanced Along Track Scanning Radiometer</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Remote-Sensing_Satellite" title="European Remote-Sensing Satellite">European Remote-Sensing Satellite</a>'s Along-Track Scanning Radiometer can measure infrared radiation emitted by fires, identifying hot spots greater than 39 °C (102 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a>'s <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hazard_Mapping_System&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hazard Mapping System (page does not exist)">Hazard Mapping System</a> combines remote-sensing data from satellite sources such as <a href="/wiki/Geostationary_Operational_Environmental_Satellite" title="Geostationary Operational Environmental Satellite">Geostationary Operational Environmental Satellite</a> (GOES), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moderate-Resolution_Imaging_Spectroradiometer" title="Moderate-Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer">Moderate-Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer</a> (MODIS), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advanced_Very_High_Resolution_Radiometer" title="Advanced Very High Resolution Radiometer">Advanced Very High Resolution Radiometer</a> (AVHRR) for detection of fire and smoke plume locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swarm_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swarm-158">[158]</a></sup> However, satellite detection is prone to offset errors, anywhere from 2 to 3 kilometers (1 to 2 mi) for MODIS and AVHRR data and up to 12 kilometers (7.5 mi) for GOES data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Satellites in geostationary orbits may become disabled, and satellites in polar orbits are often limited by their short window of observation time. Cloud cover and image resolution and may also limit the effectiveness of satellite imagery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>in 2015 a new fire detection tool is in operation at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">U.S. Department of Agriculture</a> (USDA) <a href="/wiki/United_States_Forest_Service" title="United States Forest Service">Forest Service</a> (USFS) which uses data from the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Suomi_National_Polar-orbiting_Partnership&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Suomi National Polar-orbiting Partnership (page does not exist)">Suomi National Polar-orbiting Partnership</a> (NPP) satellite to detect smaller fires in more detail than previous space-based products. The high-resolution data is used with a computer model to predict how a fire will change direction based on weather and land conditions. The active fire detection product using data from Suomi NPP's <a href="/wiki/Visible_Infrared_Imaging_Radiometer_Suite" title="Visible Infrared Imaging Radiometer Suite">Visible Infrared Imaging Radiometer Suite</a> (VIIRS) increases the resolution of fire observations to 1,230 feet (375 meters). Previous NASA satellite data products available since the early 2000s observed fires at 3,280 foot (1 kilometer) resolution. The data is one of the intelligence tools used by the USFS and Department of Interior agencies across the United States to guide resource allocation and strategic fire management decisions. The enhanced VIIRS fire product enables detection every 12 hours or less of much smaller fires and provides more detail and consistent tracking of fire lines during long duration wildfires – capabilities critical for early warning systems and support of routine mapping of fire progression. Active fire locations are available to users within minutes from the satellite overpass through data processing facilities at the USFS Remote Sensing Applications Center, which uses technologies developed by the NASA Goddard Space Flight Center Direct Readout Laboratory in Greenbelt, Maryland. The model uses data on weather conditions and the land surrounding an active fire to predict 12–18 hours in advance whether a blaze will shift direction. The state of Colorado decided to incorporate the weather-fire model in its firefighting efforts beginning with the 2016 fire season.</p>
<p>In 2014, an international campaign was organized in South Africa's Kruger National Park to validate fire detection products including the new VIIRS active fire data. In advance of that campaign, the Meraka Institute of the Council for Scientific and Industrial Research in Pretoria, South Africa, an early adopter of the VIIRS 375m fire product, put it to use during several large wildfires in Kruger.</p>
<p>The demand for timely, high-quality fire information has increased in recent years. Wildfires in the United States burn an average of 7 million acres of land each year. For the last 10 years, the USFS and Department of Interior have spent a combined average of about $2–4 billion annually on wildfire suppression.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Suppression">Suppression</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Suppression">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wildfire_suppression" title="Wildfire suppression">Wildfire suppression</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Firefighting" title="Firefighting">Firefighting</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_733844_Forest_fires_ravaging_near_Novovoronezh_Nuclear_Power_Plant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/RIAN_archive_733844_Forest_fires_ravaging_near_Novovoronezh_Nuclear_Power_Plant.jpg/220px-RIAN_archive_733844_Forest_fires_ravaging_near_Novovoronezh_Nuclear_Power_Plant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/RIAN_archive_733844_Forest_fires_ravaging_near_Novovoronezh_Nuclear_Power_Plant.jpg/330px-RIAN_archive_733844_Forest_fires_ravaging_near_Novovoronezh_Nuclear_Power_Plant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/RIAN_archive_733844_Forest_fires_ravaging_near_Novovoronezh_Nuclear_Power_Plant.jpg/440px-RIAN_archive_733844_Forest_fires_ravaging_near_Novovoronezh_Nuclear_Power_Plant.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_733844_Forest_fires_ravaging_near_Novovoronezh_Nuclear_Power_Plant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Russian firefighter extinguishing a wildfire</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wildfire suppression depends on the technologies available in the area in which the wildfire occurs. In less developed nations the techniques used can be as simple as throwing sand or beating the fire with sticks or palm fronds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> In more advanced nations, the suppression methods vary due to increased technological capacity. <a href="/wiki/Silver_iodide" title="Silver iodide">Silver iodide</a> can be used to encourage snow fall,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Fire_retardant" title="Fire retardant">fire retardants</a> and water can be dropped onto fires by <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned aerial vehicle">unmanned aerial vehicles</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airtanker" title="Airtanker">planes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Helitack" title="Helitack">helicopters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> Complete fire suppression is no longer an expectation, but the majority of wildfires are often extinguished before they grow out of control. While more than 99% of the 10,000 new wildfires each year are contained, escaped wildfires under extreme weather conditions are difficult to suppress without a change in the weather. Wildfires in Canada and the US burn an average of 54,500 square kilometers (13,000,000 acres) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>Above all, fighting wildfires can become deadly. A wildfire's burning front may also change direction unexpectedly and jump across fire breaks. Intense heat and smoke can lead to disorientation and loss of appreciation of the direction of the fire, which can make fires particularly dangerous. For example, during the 1949 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mann_Gulch_fire" title="Mann Gulch fire">Mann Gulch fire</a> in <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>, USA, thirteen <a href="/wiki/Smokejumper" title="Smokejumper">smokejumpers</a> died when they lost their communication links, became disoriented, and were overtaken by the fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> In the Australian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/February_2009_Victorian_bushfires" title="February 2009 Victorian bushfires">February 2009 Victorian bushfires</a>, at least 173 people died and over 2,029 homes and 3,500 structures were lost when they became engulfed by wildfire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-homeslost_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-homeslost-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Costs_of_wildfire_suppression">Costs of wildfire suppression</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Costs of wildfire suppression">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In California, the U.S. Forest Service spends about $200 million per year to suppress 98% of wildfires and up to $1 billion to suppress the other 2% of fires that escape initial attack and become large.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wildland_firefighting_safety">Wildland firefighting safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Wildland firefighting safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wildfire_fighter.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Wildfire_fighter.jpg/220px-Wildfire_fighter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Wildfire_fighter.jpg/330px-Wildfire_fighter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Wildfire_fighter.jpg/440px-Wildfire_fighter.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wildfire_fighter.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wildfire fighters cutting down a tree using a chainsaw</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wildland fire fighters face several life-threatening hazards including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_stress" title="Heat stress">heat stress</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fatigue_(safety)" title="Fatigue (safety)">fatigue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smoke" title="Smoke">smoke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dust" title="Dust">dust</a>, as well as the risk of other injuries such as <a href="/wiki/Burn" title="Burn">burns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wound" title="Wound">cuts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abrasion_(medical)" title="Abrasion (medical)">scrapes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_bites" title="Animal bites">animal bites</a>, and even <a href="/wiki/Rhabdomyolysis" title="Rhabdomyolysis">rhabdomyolysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niosh-blog_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-niosh-blog-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>Especially in hot weather condition, fires present the risk of heat stress, which can entail feeling heat, fatigue, weakness, vertigo, headache, or nausea. Heat stress can progress into heat strain, which entails physiological changes such as increased heart rate and core body temperature. This can lead to heat-related illnesses, such as heat rash, cramps, exhaustion or <a href="/wiki/Heat_stroke" title="Heat stroke">heat stroke</a>. Various factors can contribute to the risks posed by heat stress, including strenuous work, personal risk factors such as age and <a href="/wiki/Physical_fitness" title="Physical fitness">fitness</a>, dehydration, sleep deprivation, and burdensome <a href="/wiki/Personal_protective_equipment" title="Personal protective equipment">personal protective equipment</a>. Rest, cool water, and occasional breaks are crucial to mitigating the effects of heat stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niosh-blog_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-niosh-blog-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>Smoke, ash, and debris can also pose serious respiratory hazards to wildland fire fighters. The smoke and dust from wildfires can contain gases such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_particulate_matter" title="Atmospheric particulate matter">particulates</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Wood_ash" title="Wood ash">ash</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a>. To reduce smoke exposure, wildfire fighting crews should, whenever possible, rotate firefighters through areas of heavy smoke, avoid downwind firefighting, use equipment rather than people in holding areas, and minimize mop-up. Camps and command posts should also be located upwind of wildfires. Protective clothing and equipment can also help minimize exposure to smoke and ash.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niosh-blog_170-2"><a href="#cite_note-niosh-blog-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>Firefighters are also at risk of cardiac events including strokes and heart attacks. Fire fighters should maintain good physical fitness. Fitness programs, medical screening and examination programs which include stress tests can minimize the risks of firefighting cardiac problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niosh-blog_170-3"><a href="#cite_note-niosh-blog-170">[170]</a></sup> Other injury hazards wildland fire fighters face include slips, trips and falls, burns, scrapes and cuts from tools and equipment, being struck by trees, vehicles, or other objects, plant hazards such as thorns and poison ivy, snake and animal bites, vehicle crashes, electrocution from power lines or lightning storms, and unstable building structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-niosh-blog_170-4"><a href="#cite_note-niosh-blog-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fire_retardant">Fire retardant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Fire retardant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Fire retardants are used to slow wildfires by inhibiting combustion. They are aqueous solutions of ammonium phosphates and ammonium sulfates, as well as thickening agents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> The decision to apply retardant depends on the magnitude, location and intensity of the wildfire. In certain instances, fire retardant may also be applied as a precautionary fire defense measure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coloradoan.com_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-coloradoan.com-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>Typical fire retardants contain the same agents as fertilizers. Fire retardant may also affect water quality through leaching, eutrophication, or misapplication. Fire retardant's effects on drinking water remain inconclusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minesnewsroom.com_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-minesnewsroom.com-174">[174]</a></sup> Dilution factors, including water body size, rainfall, and water flow rates lessen the concentration and potency of fire retardant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coloradoan.com_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-coloradoan.com-173">[173]</a></sup> Wildfire debris (ash and sediment) clog rivers and reservoirs increasing the risk for floods and erosion that ultimately slow and/or damage water treatment systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-minesnewsroom.com_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-minesnewsroom.com-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> There is continued concern of fire retardant effects on land, water, wildlife habitats, and watershed quality, additional research is needed. However, on the positive side, fire retardant (specifically its nitrogen and phosphorus components) has been shown to have a fertilizing effect on nutrient-deprived soils and thus creates a temporary increase in vegetation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coloradoan.com_173-2"><a href="#cite_note-coloradoan.com-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>Current USDA procedure maintains that the aerial application of fire retardant in the United States must clear waterways by a minimum of 300 feet in order to safeguard effects of retardant runoff. Aerial uses of fire retardant are required to avoid application near waterways and endangered species (plant and animal habitats). After any incident of fire retardant misapplication, the U.S. Forest Service requires reporting and assessment impacts be made in order to determine mitigation, remediation, and/or restrictions on future retardant uses in that area.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modeling">Modeling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Modeling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wildfire_modeling" title="Wildfire modeling">Wildfire modeling</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Propagation_model_wildfire_(English).svg"><img alt="A dark region shaped like a shield with a pointed bottom. An arrow and the text &quot;propagation axis (wind)&quot; indicates a bottom-to-top direction up the body of the shield shape. The shape's pointed bottom is labeled &quot;fire start&quot;. Around the shield shape's top and thinning towards its sides, a yellow-orange region is labeled &quot;left front&quot;, &quot;right front&quot;, and (at the top) &quot;head of the fire&quot;." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="222" data-file-width="407" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Propagation_model_wildfire_%28English%29.svg/240px-Propagation_model_wildfire_%28English%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Propagation_model_wildfire_%28English%29.svg/360px-Propagation_model_wildfire_%28English%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Propagation_model_wildfire_%28English%29.svg/480px-Propagation_model_wildfire_%28English%29.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Propagation_model_wildfire_(English).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fire Propagation Model</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wildfire modeling is concerned with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Numerical_simulation" title="Numerical simulation">numerical simulation</a> of wildfires in order to comprehend and predict fire behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> Wildfire modeling aims to aid wildfire suppression, increase the safety of firefighters and the public, and minimize damage. Using <a href="/wiki/Computational_science" title="Computational science">computational science</a>, wildfire modeling involves the statistical analysis of past fire events to predict spotting risks and front behavior. Various wildfire propagation models have been proposed in the past, including simple ellipses and egg- and fan-shaped models. Early attempts to determine wildfire behavior assumed terrain and vegetation uniformity. However, the exact behavior of a wildfire's front is dependent on a variety of factors, including windspeed and slope steepness. Modern growth models utilize a combination of past ellipsoidal descriptions and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huygens%27_Principle" title="Huygens' Principle">Huygens' Principle</a> to simulate fire growth as a continuously expanding polygon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Extreme_value_theory" title="Extreme value theory">Extreme value theory</a> may also be used to predict the size of large wildfires. However, large fires that exceed suppression capabilities are often regarded as statistical outliers in standard analyses, even though fire policies are more influenced by large wildfires than by small fires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alvarado.2C_et_al..2C_66-68_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarado.2C_et_al..2C_66-68-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ACTbushfire03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="274" data-file-width="425" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/ACTbushfire03.jpg/240px-ACTbushfire03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/ACTbushfire03.jpg/360px-ACTbushfire03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/36/ACTbushfire03.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ACTbushfire03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canberra_bushfires" title="Canberra bushfires">2003 Canberra firestorm</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_risk_and_exposure">Human risk and exposure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Human risk and exposure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:941px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2009_California_Wildfires_at_JPL_-_Pasadena,_California.jpg" title="2009 California Wildfires at NASA/JPL – Pasadena, California"><img alt="" data-file-height="2110" data-file-width="9489" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/2009_California_Wildfires_at_JPL_-_Pasadena%2C_California.jpg/933px-2009_California_Wildfires_at_JPL_-_Pasadena%2C_California.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/2009_California_Wildfires_at_JPL_-_Pasadena%2C_California.jpg/1400px-2009_California_Wildfires_at_JPL_-_Pasadena%2C_California.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/2009_California_Wildfires_at_JPL_-_Pasadena%2C_California.jpg/1866px-2009_California_Wildfires_at_JPL_-_Pasadena%2C_California.jpg 2x" width="933"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2009_California_Wildfires_at_JPL_-_Pasadena,_California.jpg" title="File:2009 California Wildfires at JPL - Pasadena, California.jpg"></a></div>
2009 California Wildfires at <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">NASA/JPL</a> – Pasadena, California</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wildfire risk is the chance that a wildfire will start in or reach a particular area and the potential loss of human values if it does. Risk is dependent on variable factors such as human activities, weather patterns, availability of wildfire fuels, and the availability or lack of resources to suppress a fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> Wildfires have continually been a threat to human populations. However, human induced geographical and climatic changes are exposing populations more frequently to wildfires and increasing wildfire risk. It is speculated that the increase in wildfires arises from a century of wildfire suppression coupled with the rapid expansion of human developments into fire-prone wildlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> Wildfires are naturally occurring events that aid in promoting forest health. Global warming and climate changes are causing an increase in temperatures and more droughts nationwide which contributes to an increase in wildfire risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Airborne_hazards">Airborne hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Airborne hazards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The most noticeable adverse effect of wildfires is the destruction of property. However, the release of hazardous chemicals from the burning of wildland fuels also significantly impacts health in humans.</p>
<p>Wildfire smoke is composed primarily of carbon dioxide and water vapor. Other common smoke components present in lower concentrations are carbon monoxide, formaldehyde, acrolein, polyaromatic hydrocarbons, and benzene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oehha.ca.gov_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-oehha.ca.gov-185">[185]</a></sup> Small particulates suspended in air which come in solid form or in liquid droplets are also present in smoke. 80 -90% of wildfire smoke, by mass, is within the fine particle size class of 2.5 micrometers in diameter or smaller.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite carbon dioxide's high concentration in smoke, it poses a low health risk due to its low toxicity. Rather, carbon monoxide and fine particulate matter, particularly 2.5 µm in diameter and smaller, have been identified as the major health threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oehha.ca.gov_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-oehha.ca.gov-185">[185]</a></sup> Other chemicals are considered to be significant hazards but are found in concentrations that are too low to cause detectable health effects.</p>
<p>The degree of wildfire smoke exposure to an individual is dependent on the length, severity, duration, and proximity of the fire. People are exposed directly to smoke via the respiratory tract though inhalation of air pollutants. Indirectly, communities are exposed to wildfire debris that can contaminate soil and water supplies.</p>
<p>The U.S. Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) developed the Air Quality Index (AQI), a public resource that provides national air quality standard concentrations for common air pollutants. The public can use this index as a tool to determine their exposure to hazardous air pollutants based on visibility range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Groups_at_risk">Groups at risk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Groups at risk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Firefighters are at the greatest risk for acute and chronic health effects resulting from wildfire smoke exposure. Due to firefighters' occupational duties, they are frequently exposed to <a href="/wiki/Chemical_hazard" title="Chemical hazard">hazardous chemicals</a> at a close proximity for longer periods of time. A case study on the exposure of wildfire smoke among wildland firefighters shows that firefighters are exposed to significant levels of carbon monoxide and respiratory irritants above <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">OSHA</a>-permissible exposure limits (PEL) and ACGIH threshold limit values (TLV). 5–10% are overexposed. The study obtained exposure concentrations for one wildland firefighter over a 10-hour shift spent holding down a fireline. The firefighter was exposed to a wide range of carbon monoxide and respiratory irritant (combination of particulate matter 3.5 µm and smaller, acrolein, and formaldehype) levels. Carbon monoxide levels reached up to 160ppm and the TLV irritant index value reached a high of 10. In contrast, the OSHA PEL for carbon monoxide is 30ppm and for the TLV respiratory irritant index, the calculated threshold limit value is 1; any value above 1 exceeds exposure limits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 2001 and 2012, over 200 <a href="/wiki/Occupational_fatality" title="Occupational fatality">fatalities</a> occurred among wildland firefighters. In addition to heat and chemical hazards, firefighters are also at risk for electrocution from power lines; injuries from equipment; <a href="/wiki/Occupational_injury" title="Occupational injury">slips, trips, and falls</a>; injuries from vehicle rollovers; <a href="/wiki/Heat_illness" title="Heat illness">heat-related illness</a>; <a href="/wiki/Insect_bites_and_stings" title="Insect bites and stings">insect bites and stings</a>; <a href="/wiki/Occupational_stress" title="Occupational stress">stress</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Rhabdomyolysis" title="Rhabdomyolysis">rhabdomyolysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>Residents in communities surrounding wildfires are exposed to lower concentrations of chemicals, but they are at a greater risk for indirect exposure through water or soil contamination. Exposure to residents is greatly dependent on individual susceptibility. Vulnerable persons such as children (ages 0–4), the elderly (ages 65 and older), smokers, and pregnant women are at an increased risk due to their already compromised body systems, even when the exposures are present at low chemical concentrations and for relatively short exposure periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oehha.ca.gov_185-2"><a href="#cite_note-oehha.ca.gov-185">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additionally, there is evidence of an increase in material stress, as documented by researchers M.H. O'Donnell and A.M. Behie, thus affecting birth outcomes. In Australia, studies show that male infants born with drastically higher average birth weights were born in mostly severely fire-affected areas. This is attributed to the fact that maternal signals directly affect fetal growth patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_effects">Health effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Health effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_particulate_matter" title="Atmospheric particulate matter">Atmospheric particulate matter</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diaphragmatic_breathing.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="550" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Diaphragmatic_breathing.gif/220px-Diaphragmatic_breathing.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Diaphragmatic_breathing.gif/330px-Diaphragmatic_breathing.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Diaphragmatic_breathing.gif/440px-Diaphragmatic_breathing.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diaphragmatic_breathing.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Animation of diaphragmatic breathing with the diaphragm shown in green</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Inhalation of smoke from a wildfire can be a health hazard. Wildfire smoke is composed of carbon dioxide, water vapor, particulate matter, organic chemicals, nitrogen oxides and other compounds. The principal health concern is the inhalation of particulate matter and carbon monoxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>Particulate matter (PM) is a type of air pollution made up of particles of dust and liquid droplets. They are characterized into two categories based on the diameter of the particle. Coarse particles are between 2.5 micrometers and 10 micrometers and fine particles measure 2.5 micrometers and less. Both sizes can be inhaled. Coarse particles are filtered by the upper airways and can cause eye and sinus irritation as well as sore throat and coughing. The fine particles are more problematic because, when inhaled, they can be deposited deep into the lungs, where they are absorbed into the bloodstream. This is particularly hazardous to the very young, elderly and those with chronic conditions such as asthma, chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD), cystic fibrosis and cardiovascular conditions. The illnesses most commonly with exposure to fine particle from wildfire smoke are bronchitis, exacerbation of asthma or COPD, and pneumonia. Symptoms of these complications include wheezing and shortness of breath and cardiovascular symptoms include chest pain, rapid heart rate and fatigue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carbon monoxide (CO) is a colorless, odorless gas that can be found at the highest concentration at close proximity to a smoldering fire. For this reason, carbon monoxide inhalation is a serious threat to the health of wildfire firefighters. CO in smoke can be inhaled into the lungs where it is absorbed into the bloodstream and reduces oxygen delivery to the body's vital organs. At high concentrations, it can cause headache, weakness, dizziness, confusion, nausea, disorientation, visual impairment, coma and even death. However, even at lower concentrations, such as those found at wildfires, individuals with cardiovascular disease may experience chest pain and cardiac arrhythmia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oehha.ca.gov_185-3"><a href="#cite_note-oehha.ca.gov-185">[185]</a></sup> A recent study tracking the number and cause of wildfire firefighter deaths from 1990–2006 found that 21.9% of the deaths occurred from heart attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another important and somewhat less obvious health effect of wildfires is psychiatric diseases and disorders. Both adults and children from countries ranging from the United States and Canada to Greece and Australia who were directly and indirectly affected by wildfires were found by researchers to demonstrate several different mental conditions linked to their experience with the wildfires. These include post-traumatic stress disorder (PTSD), depression, anxiety, and phobias.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a new twist to wildfire health effects, former uranium mining sites were burned over in the summer of 2012 near North Fork, Idaho. This prompted concern from area residents and Idaho State Department of Environmental Quality officials over the potential spread of radiation in the resultant smoke, since those sites had never been completely cleaned up from radioactive remains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The EPA has defined acceptable concentrations of particulate matter in the air, through the National Ambient Air Quality Standards and monitoring of ambient air quality has been mandated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> Due to these monitoring programs and the incidence of several large wildfires near populated areas, epidemiological studies have been conducted and demonstrate an association between human health effects and an increase in fine particulate matter due to wildfire smoke.</p>
<p>An increase in PM emitted from the Hayman fire in Colorado in June 2002, was associated with an increase in respiratory symptoms in patients with COPD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> Looking at the wildfires in Southern California in October 2003 in a similar manner, investigators have shown an increase in hospital admissions due to asthma during peak concentrations of PM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> Children participating in the Children's Health Study were also found to have an increase in eye and respiratory symptoms, medication use and physician visits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> Recently, it was demonstrated that mothers who were pregnant during the fires gave birth to babies with a slightly reduced average birth weight compared to those who were not exposed to wildfire during birth. Suggesting that pregnant women may also be at greater risk to adverse effects from wildfire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> Worldwide it is estimated that 339,000 people die due to the effects of wildfire smoke each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_wildfires">Notable wildfires</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wildfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Notable wildfires">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-Pausas_594-110"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Pausas_594_110-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Pausas_594_110-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Pausas and Keeley, 594</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-cenozoic-division-111"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-cenozoic-division_111-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Historically, the <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> has been divided up into the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary" title="Quaternary">Quaternary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tertiary" title="Tertiary">Tertiary</a> sub-eras, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Neogene" title="Neogene">Neogene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a> periods. The <a class="external text" href="http://www.stratigraphy.org/upload/ISChart2009.pdf" rel="nofollow">2009 version of the ICS time chart</a> recognizes a slightly extended Quaternary as well as the Paleogene and a truncated Neogene, the Tertiary having been demoted to informal status.</span></li>
